//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/40px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Ocean</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A body of water that composes much of a planet\'s hydrosphere</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a></div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Ocean_(disambiguation)" title="Ocean (disambiguation)">Ocean (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2513" data-file-width="3755" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/220px-Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/330px-Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/440px-Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clouds_over_the_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clouds over the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a></div></div></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:auto;"><tbody><tr><td style="padding:0.4em 0;background:#ddeffa;padding:0.5em 1.0em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceanus.png" title="View of the Earth where all five oceans visible"><img alt="View of the Earth where all five oceans visible" data-file-height="407" data-file-width="407" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Oceanus.png/120px-Oceanus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Oceanus.png/180px-Oceanus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Oceanus.png/240px-Oceanus.png 2x" width="120"/></a></td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em">Earth\'s <a class="mw-selflink selflink">oceans</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;font-weight:normal;">\n<hr/><a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a><hr/></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Five_oceans" title="Template:Five oceans"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Five_oceans" title="Template talk:Five oceans"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Five_oceans&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>An <b>ocean</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BD%A8%CE%BA%CE%B5%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:&#8040;&#954;&#949;&#945;&#957;&#972;&#962;">&#8040;&#954;&#949;&#945;&#957;&#972;&#962;</a>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">transc.</a></small><i>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Okean&#243;s</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tufts.pers_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-tufts.pers-1">[1]</a></sup>) is a body of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> that composes much of a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pri.ocean_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-pri.ocean-2">[2]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, an ocean is one of the major conventional divisions of the <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a>. These are, in descending order by area, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern</a> (Antarctic), and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic</a> Oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed.130201_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed.130201-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw.ocean_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw.ocean-4">[4]</a></sup> The word "ocean" is often used interchangeably with "sea" in <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a>. Strictly speaking, a <i><a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a></i> is a body of water (generally a division of the world ocean) partly or fully enclosed by land,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pri.sea_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-pri.sea-5">[5]</a></sup> though "the sea" refers also to the oceans.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">Saline water</a> covers approximately 361,000,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (139,000,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) and is customarily divided into several principal oceans and smaller seas, with the ocean covering approximately 71% of Earth\'s surface and 90% of the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa.gov_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaa.gov-6">[6]</a></sup> The ocean contains 97% of Earth\'s water, and <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanographers</a> have stated that less than 5% of the World Ocean has been explored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa.gov_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-noaa.gov-6">[6]</a></sup> The total volume is approximately 1.35 billion cubic kilometers (320 million cu mi) with an average depth of nearly 3,700 meters (12,100&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hypertextbook2003_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-hypertextbook2003-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charette_Smith_Ocean-depth_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charette_Smith_Ocean-depth-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the world ocean is the principal component of Earth\'s hydrosphere, it is integral to <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>, forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, and influences <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> patterns. The World Ocean is the <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> of 230,000 known <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, but because much of it is unexplored, the number of species that exist in the ocean is much larger, possibly over two million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-la.2009_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-la.2009-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">origin of Earth\'s oceans</a> is unknown; oceans are thought to have formed in the <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">Hadean</a> eon and may have been the impetus for the <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">emergence of life</a>.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_oceans" title="Extraterrestrial oceans">Extraterrestrial oceans</a> may be composed of water or other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>. The only confirmed large stable bodies of extraterrestrial surface <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquids" title="Liquids">liquids</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">lakes of Titan</a>, although there is evidence for the existence of oceans elsewhere in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Early in their geologic histories, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> are theorized to have had large water oceans. The <a href="/wiki/Mars_ocean_hypothesis" title="Mars ocean hypothesis">Mars ocean hypothesis</a> suggests that nearly a third of the surface of Mars was once covered by water, and a <a href="/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect" title="Runaway greenhouse effect">runaway greenhouse effect</a> may have boiled away the global ocean of Venus. Compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> dissolved in water lower its freezing point so that water might exist in large quantities in extraterrestrial environments as brine or convecting <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>. Unconfirmed oceans are speculated beneath the surface of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwarf_planets" title="Dwarf planets">dwarf planets</a> and natural satellites; notably, the ocean of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> is estimated to have over twice the water volume of Earth. The Solar System\'s <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a> are also thought to have liquid <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheric</a> layers of yet to be confirmed compositions. Oceans may also exist on <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exomoon" title="Exomoon">exomoons</a>, including surface oceans of liquid water within a <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone" title="Circumstellar habitable zone">circumstellar habitable zone</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ocean_planet" title="Ocean planet">Ocean planets</a> are a hypothetical type of planet with a surface completely covered with liquid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mindblowingscience_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-mindblowingscience-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctext">Extrasolar</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Non-water_surface_liquids"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Non-water surface liquids</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<p>The word &#171;&#160;ocean&#160;&#187; comes from the figure in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/o&#650;/: \'o\' in \'code\'">o&#650;</span><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="/i&#720;/: \'ee\' in \'fleece\'">i&#720;</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BD%A8%CE%BA%CE%B5%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:&#8040;&#954;&#949;&#945;&#957;&#972;&#962;">&#8040;&#954;&#949;&#945;&#957;&#972;&#962;</a></span> <i>&#332;kean&#243;s</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <small>pronounced&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">[&#596;&#720;kean&#243;s]</a></span>), the elder of the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> in classical <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, believed by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> to be the divine personification of the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, an enormous <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> encircling the world.\n</p><p>The concept of &#332;kean&#243;s has an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_religion" title="Indo-European religion">Indo-European</a> connection. Greek &#332;k\n</p><p>ean&#243;s has been compared to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vedic" title="Vedic">Vedic</a> epithet &#257;-&#347;&#225;y&#257;na-, predicated of the dragon V&#7771;tra-, who captured the cows/rivers. Related to this notion, the Okeanos is represented with a dragon-tail on some early Greek vases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Earth.27s_global_ocean"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth\'s_global_ocean">Earth\'s global ocean</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_ocean_map.gif"><img alt="Rotating series of maps showing alternate divisions of the oceans" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/World_ocean_map.gif/240px-World_ocean_map.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/World_ocean_map.gif/360px-World_ocean_map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/World_ocean_map.gif/480px-World_ocean_map.gif 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_ocean_map.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various ways to divide the World Ocean</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanic_divisions">Oceanic divisions</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_oceans" title="Borders of the oceans">Borders of the oceans</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Layers_of_Ocean_(1).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Layers_of_Ocean_%281%29.svg/220px-Layers_of_Ocean_%281%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Layers_of_Ocean_%281%29.svg/330px-Layers_of_Ocean_%281%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Layers_of_Ocean_%281%29.svg/440px-Layers_of_Ocean_%281%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Layers_of_Ocean_(1).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1. Epipelagic zone: surface &#8211; 200 meters deep 2. Mesopelagic zone: 200&#160;m &#8211; 1000&#160;m 3. Bathypelagic zone: 1000&#160;m &#8211; 4000&#160;m 4. Abyssopelagic zone: 4000&#160;m &#8211; 6000&#160;m 5. Hadal zone (the trenches): 6000&#160;m to the bottom of the ocean</div></div></div>\n<p>Though generally described as several separate oceans, the global, interconnected body of salt water is sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean or global ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAoO_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNAoO-16">[16]</a></sup> The concept of a continuous body of water with relatively free interchange among its parts is of fundamental importance to <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spil.42_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-spil.42-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The major oceanic divisions &#8211; listed below in descending order of area and volume &#8211; are defined in part by the <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a>, and other criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="margin: 0.2em auto; text-align: right">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th class="unsortable" width="22">#</th>\n<th>Ocean</th>\n<th (Tomczak, 2005),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclop&#230;dia Britannica">Encyclop&#230;dia Britannica</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc-brit_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-encyc-brit-25">[25]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">International Telecommunication Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itu_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-itu-27">[27]</a></sup></div>\n<p>Oceans are fringed by smaller, adjoining bodies of water such as <a href="/wiki/List_of_seas" title="List of seas">seas</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_gulfs" title="List of gulfs">gulfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bay" title="Bay">bays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bight_(geography)" title="Bight (geography)">bights</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strait" title="Strait">straits</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_system">Global system</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="964" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif/300px-World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif/450px-World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif/600px-World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Distribution_of_Mid-Oceanic_Ridges.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World Distribution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-oceanic_ridge" title="Mid-oceanic ridge">Mid-Oceanic Ridges</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USGS" title="USGS">USGS</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="775" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/250px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/375px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/500px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three main types of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate</a> boundaries.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">mid-ocean ridges</a> of the world are connected and form a single global mid-oceanic ridge system that is part of every ocean and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_mountain_ranges#By_length" title="List of mountain ranges">longest</a> <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain range</a> in the world. The continuous mountain range is 65,000&#160;km (40,000&#160;mi) long (several times longer than the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>, the longest continental mountain range).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a></div>\n<p>The total mass of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a> is about 1.4 quintillion metric tons (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7018140000000000000&#9824;</span>1.4<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>18</sup></span> long tons or <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7018150000000000000&#9824;</span>1.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>18</sup></span> short tons), which is about 0.023% of Earth\'s total mass. Less than 3% is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a>; the rest is <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">saltwater</a>, almost all of which is in the ocean. The area of the World Ocean is about 361.9 million square kilometers (139.7 million square miles),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-9">[9]</a></sup> which covers about 70.9% of Earth\'s surface, and its volume is approximately 1.335 billion cubic kilometers (320.3 million cubic miles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-9">[9]</a></sup> This can be thought of as a cube of water with an edge length of 1,101 kilometers (684&#160;mi). Its average depth is about 3,688 meters (12,100&#160;ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-9">[9]</a></sup> and its maximum depth is 10,994 meters (6.831&#160;mi) at the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smmt_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-smmt-29">[29]</a></sup> Nearly half of the world\'s marine waters are over 3,000 meters (9,800&#160;ft) deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAoO_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNAoO-16">[16]</a></sup> The vast expanses of deep ocean (anything below 200 meters or 660 feet) cover about 66% of Earth\'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drazen_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-drazen-30">[30]</a></sup> This does not include seas not connected to the World Ocean, such as the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.\n</p><p>The bluish <a href="/wiki/Ocean_color" title="Ocean color">ocean color</a> is a composite of several contributing agents. Prominent contributors include dissolved organic matter and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coble_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coble-31">[31]</a></sup> Mariners and other seafarers have reported that the ocean <a href="/wiki/Milky_seas_effect" title="Milky seas effect">often emits a visible glow</a> which extends for miles at night. In 2005, scientists announced that for the first time, they had obtained photographic evidence of this glow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ls.051004_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ls.051004-32">[32]</a></sup> It is most likely caused by <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usa.2005_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-usa.2005-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ns.18825195_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ns.18825195-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.algae_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.algae-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanic_zones">Oceanic zones</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_divisions.svg"><img alt="Drawing showing divisions according to depth and distance from shore" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="835" data-file-width="1114" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Oceanic_divisions.svg/400px-Oceanic_divisions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Oceanic_divisions.svg/600px-Oceanic_divisions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Oceanic_divisions.svg/800px-Oceanic_divisions.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_divisions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The major oceanic zones, based on depth and biophysical conditions</div></div></div>\n<p>Oceanographers divide the ocean into different vertical <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_zone" title="Oceanic zone">zones</a> defined by physical and biological conditions. The <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_zone" title="Pelagic zone">pelagic zone</a> includes all open ocean regions, and can be divided into further regions categorized by depth and light abundance. The <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic zone</a> includes the oceans from the surface to a depth of 200&#160;m; it is the region where photosynthesis can occur and is, therefore, the most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiverse" title="Biodiverse">biodiverse</a>. Because plants require <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, life found deeper than the photic zone must either rely on material sinking from above (see <a href="/wiki/Marine_snow" title="Marine snow">marine snow</a>) or find another energy source. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vents" title="Hydrothermal vents">Hydrothermal vents</a> are the primary source of energy in what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Aphotic_zone" title="Aphotic zone">aphotic zone</a> (depths exceeding 200&#160;m). The pelagic part of the photic zone is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epipelagic" title="Epipelagic">epipelagic</a>.\n</p><p>The pelagic part of the aphotic zone can be further divided into vertical regions according to temperature.\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopelagic" title="Mesopelagic">mesopelagic</a> is the uppermost region. Its lowermost boundary is at a <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a> of 12&#160;&#176;C (54&#160;&#176;F), which, in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> generally lies at 700&#8211;1,000 meters (2,300&#8211;3,300&#160;ft). Next is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathypelagic" title="Bathypelagic">bathypelagic</a> lying between 10 and 4&#160;&#176;C (50 and 39&#160;&#176;F), typically between 700&#8211;1,000 meters (2,300&#8211;3,300&#160;ft) and 2,000&#8211;4,000 meters (6,600&#8211;13,100&#160;ft), lying along the top of the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plain</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_zone" title="Abyssal zone">abyssopelagic</a>, whose lower boundary lies at about 6,000 meters (20,000&#160;ft). The last zone includes the deep <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">oceanic trench</a>, and is known as the <a href="/wiki/Hadal_zone" title="Hadal zone">hadalpelagic</a>. This lies between 6,000&#8211;11,000 meters (20,000&#8211;36,000&#160;ft) and is the deepest oceanic zone.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a> zones are aphotic and correspond to the three deepest zones of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep-sea" title="Deep-sea">deep-sea</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bathyal_zone" title="Bathyal zone">bathyal zone</a> covers the continental slope down to about 4,000 meters (13,000&#160;ft). The abyssal zone covers the abyssal plains between 4,000 and 6,000&#160;m. Lastly, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadal" title="Hadal">hadal</a> zone corresponds to the hadalpelagic zone, which is found in oceanic trenches.\n</p><p>The pelagic zone can be further subdivided into two subregions: the <a href="/wiki/Neritic_zone" title="Neritic zone">neritic zone</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_zone" title="Oceanic zone">oceanic zone</a>. The neritic zone encompasses the water mass directly above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_shelves" title="Continental shelves">continental shelves</a> whereas the oceanic zone includes all the completely open water.\n</p><p>In contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">littoral zone</a> covers the region between low and high tide and represents the transitional area between marine and terrestrial conditions. It is also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertidal" title="Intertidal">intertidal</a> zone because it is the area where tide level affects the conditions of the region.\n</p><p>If a zone undergoes dramatic changes in temperature with depth, it contains a <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a>. The tropical thermocline is typically deeper than the thermocline at higher latitudes. Polar waters, which receive relatively little solar energy, are not stratified by temperature and generally lack a thermocline because surface water at polar latitudes are nearly as cold as water at greater depths. Below the thermocline, water is very cold, ranging from &#8722;1&#160;&#176;C to 3&#160;&#176;C. Because this deep and cold layer contains the bulk of ocean water, the average temperature of the world ocean is 3.9&#160;&#176;C.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\nIf a zone undergoes dramatic changes in salinity with depth, it contains a <a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a>. If a zone undergoes a strong, vertical chemistry gradient with depth, it contains a <a href="/wiki/Chemocline" title="Chemocline">chemocline</a>.\n</p><p>The halocline often coincides with the thermocline, and the combination produces a pronounced pycnocline.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_gravity_map.gif"><img alt="False color photo" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="1010" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Ocean_gravity_map.gif/350px-Ocean_gravity_map.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Ocean_gravity_map.gif/525px-Ocean_gravity_map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Ocean_gravity_map.gif/700px-Ocean_gravity_map.gif 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_gravity_map.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of large underwater features (1995, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NOAA" title="NOAA">NOAA</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The deepest point in the ocean is the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a>, located in the Pacific Ocean near the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mariana_Islands" title="Northern Mariana Islands">Northern Mariana Islands</a>. Its maximum depth has been estimated to be 10,971 meters (35,994&#160;ft) (plus or minus 11 meters; see the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a> article for discussion of the various estimates of the maximum depth.) The British naval vessel <i>Challenger II</i> surveyed the trench in 1951 and named the deepest part of the trench the "<a href="/wiki/Challenger_Deep" title="Challenger Deep">Challenger Deep</a>". In 1960, the <a href="/wiki/Bathyscaphe_Trieste" title="Bathyscaphe Trieste">Trieste</a> successfully reached the bottom of the trench, manned by a crew of two men.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanic_maritime_currents">Oceanic maritime currents</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_currents_1943.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1406" data-file-width="2639" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Ocean_currents_1943.jpg/250px-Ocean_currents_1943.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Ocean_currents_1943.jpg/375px-Ocean_currents_1943.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Ocean_currents_1943.jpg/500px-Ocean_currents_1943.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_currents_1943.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oceanic surface currents (U.S. Army, 1943).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M2_tidal_constituent.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="730" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/M2_tidal_constituent.jpg/250px-M2_tidal_constituent.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/M2_tidal_constituent.jpg/375px-M2_tidal_constituent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/M2_tidal_constituent.jpg/500px-M2_tidal_constituent.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M2_tidal_constituent.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amphidromic points showing the direction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tides" title="Tides">tides</a> per incrementation periods along with resonating directions of wavelength movements.</div></div></div>\n<p>Oceanic maritime currents have different origins. Tidal currents are in phase with the <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tide</a>, hence are <a href="/wiki/Quasiperiodicity" title="Quasiperiodicity">quasiperiodic</a>, they may form various knots in certain places,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> most notably around <a href="/wiki/Cape_(geography)" title="Cape (geography)">headlands</a>. Non periodic currents have for origin the waves, wind and different densities.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> create surface currents (designated as &#171;&#160;drift currents&#160;&#187;). These currents can decompose in one quasi permanent current (which varies within the hourly scale) and one movement of <a href="/wiki/Stokes_drift" title="Stokes drift">Stokes drift</a> under the effect of rapid waves movement (at the echelon of a couple of seconds).).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The quasi permanent current is accelerated by the breaking of waves, and in a lesser governing effect, by the friction of the wind on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This acceleration of the current takes place in the direction of waves and dominant wind. Accordingly, when the sea depth increases, the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth\'s rotation">rotation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">earth</a> changes the direction of currents, in proportion with the increase of depth while friction lowers their speed. At a certain sea depth, the current changes direction and is seen inverted in the opposite direction with speed current becoming nul: known as the <a href="/wiki/Ekman_spiral" title="Ekman spiral">Ekman spiral</a>. The influence of these currents is mainly experienced at the mixed layer of the ocean surface, often from 400 to 800 meters of maximum depth. These currents can considerably alter, change and are dependent on the various yearly seasons. If the mixed layer is less thick (10 to 20 meters), the quasi permanent current at the surface adopts an extreme oblique direction in relation to the direction of the wind, becoming virtually homogeneous, until the <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">Thermocline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Deep_sea" title="Deep sea">deep</a> however, maritime currents are caused by the temperature gradients and the <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> between water density masses.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">littoral zones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breaking_wave" title="Breaking wave">breaking wave</a> is so intense and the depth measurement so low, that maritime currents reach often 1 to 2 knots.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png"><img alt="World map with colored, directed lines showing how water moves through the oceans. Cold deep water rises and warms in the central Pacific and in the Indian, whereas warm water sinks and cools near Greenland in the North Atlantic and near Antarctica in the South Atlantic." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/200px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/300px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/400px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the global thermohaline circulation; blue represent deep-water currents, whereas red represent surface currents</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">Ocean currents</a> greatly affect Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> by transferring heat from the tropics to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_regions" title="Polar regions">polar regions</a>. Transferring warm or cold air and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> to coastal regions, winds may carry them inland. Surface heat and freshwater <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">fluxes</a> create global <a href="/wiki/Density_gradient" title="Density gradient">density gradients</a> that drive the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> part of large-scale ocean circulation. It plays an important role in supplying heat to the polar regions, and thus in sea ice regulation. Changes in the thermohaline circulation are thought to have significant impacts on <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget" title="Earth\'s energy budget">Earth\'s energy budget</a>. In so far as the thermohaline circulation governs the rate at which deep waters reach the surface, it may also significantly influence atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> concentrations.\n</p><p>For a discussion of the possibilities of changes to the thermohaline circulation under <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Shutdown_of_thermohaline_circulation" title="Shutdown of thermohaline circulation">shutdown of thermohaline circulation</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">Antarctic Circumpolar Current</a> encircles that continent, influencing the area\'s climate and connecting currents in several oceans.\n</p><p>One of the most dramatic forms of <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> occurs over the oceans: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a> (also called "typhoons" and "hurricanes" depending upon where the system forms).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">Marine biology</a></div>\n<p>The ocean has a significant effect on the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. Oceanic <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, as a phase of the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>, is the source of most rainfall, and ocean temperatures determine <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> patterns that affect life on land. Life within the ocean <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> 3 billion years prior to life on land. Both the depth and the distance from shore strongly influence the <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> of the plants and animals present in each region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcc.34_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcc.34-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As it is thought that life evolved in the ocean, the diversity of life is immense, including:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a>&#160;: ubiquitous single-celled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotes" title="Prokaryotes">prokaryotes</a> found throughout the world</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a>&#160;: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotes" title="Prokaryotes">prokaryotes</a> distinct from bacteria, that inhabit many environments of the ocean, as well as many <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extreme environments</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">Algae</a>&#160;: algae is a "catch-all" term to include many <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">single-celled</a> <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatoms</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinoflagellates" title="Dinoflagellates">dinoflagellates</a>, but also multicellular algae, such as some <a href="/wiki/Red_algae" title="Red algae">red algae</a> (including organisms like <a href="/wiki/Pyropia" title="Pyropia">Pyropia</a>, which is the source of the edible <a href="/wiki/Nori" title="Nori">nori</a> seaweed), and <a href="/wiki/Brown_algae" title="Brown algae">brown algae</a> (including organisms like <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>).</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">Plants</a>&#160;: including <a href="/wiki/Seagrass" title="Seagrass">sea grasses</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mangroves" title="Mangroves">mangroves</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">Fungi</a>&#160;: many <a href="/wiki/Marine_fungi" title="Marine fungi">marine fungi</a> with diverse roles are found in oceanic environments</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals" title="Animals">Animals</a>&#160;: most animal <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> have species that inhabit the ocean, including many that are only found in marine environments such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porifera" title="Porifera">sponges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">Cnidaria</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ctenophora" title="Ctenophora">comb jellies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">Brachiopods</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">Echinoderms</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_star" title="Sea star">sea stars</a>). Many other familiar animal groups primarily live in the ocean, including <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a> (includes <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>), <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> (includes <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobsters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>), <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharks" title="Sharks">sharks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a> (includes <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a>).</li></ul>\n<p>In addition, many land animals have adapted to living a major part of their life on the oceans. For instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seabirds" title="Seabirds">seabirds</a> are a diverse group of birds that have adapted to a life mainly on the oceans. They feed on marine animals and spend most of their lifetime on water, many only going on land for breeding. Other birds that have adapted to oceans as their living space are <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">seagulls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pelecaniformes" title="Pelecaniformes">pelicans</a>. Seven species of turtles, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a>, also spend most of their time in the oceans.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gases">Gases</span></h3>\n<table cellpadding="5" class="wikitable" style="margin:auto;">\n<caption>Characteristics of oceanic gases <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soest.OCN623_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-soest.OCN623-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uiuc.L23_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-uiuc.L23-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sea.water_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-sea.water-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Gas</th>\n<th>Concentration of seawater, by mass (in parts per million), for the whole ocean</th>\n<th>% Dissolved gas, by volume, in seawater at the ocean surface\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>)</b></td>\n<td>64 to 107</td>\n<td>15%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Nitrogen (N<sub>2</sub>)</b></td>\n<td>10 to 18</td>\n<td>48%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Oxygen (O<sub>2</sub>)</b></td>\n<td>0 to 13</td>\n<td>36%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption><b>Solubility of oceanic gases (in mL/L) with temperature at salinity of 33&#8240; and atmospheric pressure</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit.12.742_43-0"><a class="mw-headline" id="Surface">Surface</span></h3>\n<table cellpadding="5" class="wikitable" style="margin:auto;">\n<caption>Generalized characteristics of ocean surface by latitude <sup 5&#160;&#176;C\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixing_time">Mixing time</span></h3>\n<table cellpadding="5" class="wikitable" style="margin:auto;">\n<caption>Mean (Cl<sup>&#8722;</sup>)</td>\n<td>100,000,000\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salinity">Salinity</span></h3>\n<p>A zone of rapid salinity increase with depth is called a <a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a>. The temperature of maximum density of <a href="/wiki/Salinity#Seawater" title="Salinity">seawater</a> decreases as its salt content increases. Freezing temperature of water decreases with salinity, and boiling temperature of water increases with <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a>. Typical seawater freezes at around &#8722;2&#160;&#176;C at <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> If precipitation exceeds evaporation, as is the case in polar and temperate regions, salinity will be lower. If evaporation exceeds precipitation, as is the case in tropical regions, salinity will be higher. Thus, oceanic waters in polar regions have lower salinity content than oceanic waters in temperate and tropical regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Salinity can be calculated using the chlorinity, which is a measure of the total mass of halogen ions (includes fluorine, chlorine, bromine, and iodine) in seawater. By international agreement, the following formula is used to determine salinity:\n</p><p><b>Salinity (in &#8240;) = 1.80655 &#215; Chlorinity (in &#8240;)</b>\n</p><p>The average chlorinity is about 19.2&#8240;, and, thus, the average salinity is around 34.7&#8240; <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absorption_of_light">Absorption of light</span></h3>\n<table cellpadding="5" class="wikitable" style="margin:auto;">\n<caption>Absorption of light in different wavelengths by ocean <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Color: Wavelength (nm)</th>\n<th>Depth at which 99 percent of the wavelength is absorbed (in meters)</th>\n<th>Percent absorbed in 1 meter of water\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Ultraviolet (UV)</b>: 310</td>\n<td>31</td>\n<td>14.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Violet (V)</b>: 400</td>\n<td>107</td>\n<td>4.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Blue (B)</b>: 475</td>\n<td>254</td>\n<td>1.8\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Green (G)</b>: 525</td>\n<td>113</td>\n<td>4.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Yellow (Y)</b>: 575</td>\n<td>51</td>\n<td>8.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Orange (O)</b>: 600</td>\n<td>25</td>\n<td>16.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Red (R)</b>: 725</td>\n<td>4</td>\n<td>71.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Infrared (IR)</b>: 800</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td>82.0\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_value">Economic value</span></h3>\n<p>Many of the world\'s goods are moved by <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ship</a> between the world\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaport" title="Seaport">seaports</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Oceans are also the major supply source for the <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">fishing industry</a>. Some of the major harvests are <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crabs" title="Crabs">crabs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa.gov_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-noaa.gov-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waves_and_swell">Waves and swell</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">Wind wave</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sea#Waves" title="Sea">Sea &#167;&#160;Waves</a></div>\n<p>The motions of the ocean surface, known as undulations or <i><a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">waves</a></i>, are the partial and alternate rising and falling of the ocean surface. The series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_waves" title="Mechanical waves">mechanical waves</a> that propagate along the interface between water and air is called <a href="/wiki/Swell_(ocean)" title="Swell (ocean)">swell</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_oceans">Extraterrestrial oceans</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_liquid_water" title="Extraterrestrial liquid water">Extraterrestrial liquid water</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_lakes_and_seas_in_the_Solar_System" title="List of largest lakes and seas in the Solar System">List of largest lakes and seas in the Solar System</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2812" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg/300px-PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg/450px-PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg/600px-PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s conception of subsurface ocean of Enceladus confirmed April 3, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140404_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140404-57">[57]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EuropaInterior1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1636" data-file-width="1192" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/EuropaInterior1.jpg/220px-EuropaInterior1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/EuropaInterior1.jpg/330px-EuropaInterior1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/EuropaInterior1.jpg/440px-EuropaInterior1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EuropaInterior1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two models for the composition of Europa predict a large subsurface ocean of liquid water. Similar models have been proposed for other celestial bodies in the Solar System.</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Earth is the only known <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> with large stable bodies of liquid water on its surface and the only one in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, other celestial bodies are thought to have large oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150407_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150407-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planets">Planets</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, are thought to lack surfaces and instead have a stratum of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a>; however their <a href="/wiki/Planetary_geology" title="Planetary geology">planetary geology</a> is not well understood. The possibility of the <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a> <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> having hot, highly compressed, <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> water under their thick atmospheres has been hypothesised. Although their composition is still not fully understood, a 2006 study by Wiktorowicz and Ingersall ruled out the possibility of such a water "ocean" existing on Neptune,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WiktorowiczIngersoll2007_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-WiktorowiczIngersoll2007-59">[59]</a></sup> though some studies have suggested that exotic oceans of liquid <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> are possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silvera2010_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silvera2010-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mars_ocean_hypothesis" title="Mars ocean hypothesis">Mars ocean hypothesis</a> suggests that nearly a third of the surface of Mars was once covered by water, though the water on Mars is no longer oceanic (much of it residing in the <a href="/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps" title="Martian polar ice caps">ice caps</a>). The possibility continues to be studied along with reasons for their apparent disappearance. Astronomers think that <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">Venus</a> had liquid water and perhaps oceans in its very early history.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> If they existed, all later vanished via <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Venus" title="Geology of Venus">resurfacing</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_satellites">Natural satellites</span></h3>\n<p>A global layer of liquid water thick enough to decouple the crust from the mantle is thought to be present on the <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a> <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a> and, with less certainty, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clubsandwich_2014_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-clubsandwich_2014-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vance_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vance-62">[62]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncycSolSys-Triton_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncycSolSys-Triton-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ocean_triton_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ocean_triton-64">[64]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> ocean is thought to be present on <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>. <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">Geysers</a> have been found on Saturn\'s moon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>, possibly originating from an ocean about 10 kilometers (6.2&#160;mi) beneath the surface ice shell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-56">[56]</a></sup> Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icy_moons" title="Icy moons">icy moons</a> may also have internal oceans, or may once have had internal oceans that have now frozen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">bodies of liquid hydrocarbons</a> are thought to be present on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, although they are not large enough to be considered oceans and are sometimes referred to as <i>lakes</i> or seas. The <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a> space mission initially discovered only what appeared to be dry lakebeds and empty river channels, suggesting that Titan had lost what surface liquids it might have had. Later flybys of Titan provided radar and infrared images that showed a series of hydrocarbon lakes in the colder polar regions. Titan is thought to have a subsurface liquid-water ocean under the ice in addition to the hydrocarbon mix that forms atop its outer crust.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dwarf_planets_and_trans-Neptunian_objects">Dwarf planets and trans-Neptunian objects</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="963" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg/220px-Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg/330px-Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg/440px-Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Cutaway-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram showing a possible internal structure of <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> appears to be <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiated</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">core</a> and icy <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> and may harbour a liquid-water ocean under its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccord.2005_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-mccord.2005-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cr.ceres_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-cr.ceres-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Not enough is known of the larger <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian objects</a> to determine whether they are differentiated bodies capable of supporting oceans, although models of radioactive decay suggest that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pluto_(dwarf_planet)" title="Pluto (dwarf planet)">Pluto</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluto-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a> have oceans beneath solid icy crusts approximately <span class="nowrap">100 to 180 km</span> thick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extrasolar">Extrasolar</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="735" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg/220px-UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg/330px-UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg/440px-UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UpsilonAndromedae_D_moons.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rendering of a hypothetical large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_moon" title="Extrasolar moon">extrasolar moon</a> with surface liquid-water oceans</div></div></div>\n<p>Some planets and natural satellites outside the Solar System are likely to have oceans, including possible water ocean planets similar to Earth in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a> or "liquid-water belt". The detection of oceans, even through the <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> method, however is likely extremely difficult and inconclusive.\n</p><p>Theoretical models have been used to predict with high probability that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_1214_b" title="GJ 1214 b">GJ 1214 b</a>, detected by transit, is composed of exotic form of <a href="/wiki/Ice_VII" title="Ice VII">ice VII</a>, making up 75% of its mass,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfa.pr200924_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-cfa.pr200924-69">[69]</a></sup>\nmaking it an <a href="/wiki/Ocean_planet" title="Ocean planet">ocean planet</a>.\n</p><p>Other possible candidates are merely speculated based on their mass and position in the habitable zone include planet though little is actually known of their composition. Some scientists speculate <a href="/wiki/Kepler-22b" title="Kepler-22b">Kepler-22b</a> may be an "ocean-like" planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upr.exo_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-upr.exo-70">[70]</a></sup> Models have been proposed for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliese_581_d" title="Gliese 581 d">Gliese 581 d</a> that could include surface oceans. <a href="/wiki/Gliese_436_b" title="Gliese 436 b">Gliese 436 b</a> is speculated to have an ocean of "hot ice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r.20070516_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-r.20070516-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Exomoon" title="Exomoon">Exomoons</a> orbiting planets, particularly gas giants within their parent star\'s habitable zone may theoretically have surface oceans.\n</p><p>Terrestrial planets will acquire water during their accretion, some of which will be buried in the magma ocean but most of it will go into a steam atmosphere, and when the atmosphere cools it will collapse on to the surface forming an ocean. There will also be outgassing of water from the mantle as the magma solidifies&#8212;this will happen even for planets with a low percentage of their mass composed of water, so "super-Earth exoplanets may be expected to commonly produce water oceans within tens to hundreds of millions of years of their last major accretionary impact."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ass.332_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-ass.332-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-water_surface_liquids">Non-water surface liquids</span></h3>\n<p>Oceans, seas, lakes and other bodies of liquids can be composed of liquids other than water, for example the <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> lakes on <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. The possibility of seas of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> on <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> was also considered but ruled out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyc-73">[73]</a></sup> There is evidence that the icy surfaces of the moons <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, Titan and <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a> are shells floating on oceans of very dense liquid water or water&#8211;ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agu.p21a_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-agu.p21a-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ic.208_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ic.208-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar.0032_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-ar.0032-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cop.213_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-cop.213-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucsc.2008_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucsc.2008-78">[78]</a></sup> Earth is often called <i>the</i> ocean planet because it is 70% covered in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nih.12349465_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-nih.12349465-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci.1998_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci.1998-80">[80]</a></sup> Extrasolar <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> that are extremely close to their parent star will be <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a> and so one half of the planet will be a magma ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar.1204_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ar.1204-81">[81]</a></sup> It is also possible that terrestrial planets had magma oceans at some point during their formation as a result of giant impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmsu.oem_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-nmsu.oem-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hot_Neptune" title="Hot Neptune">Hot Neptunes</a> close to their star could lose their atmospheres via <a href="/wiki/Hydrodynamic_escape" title="Hydrodynamic escape">hydrodynamic escape</a>, leaving behind their cores with various liquids on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epsc.542_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-epsc.542-83">[83]</a></sup> Where there are suitable temperatures and pressures, volatile chemicals that might exist as liquids in abundant quantities on planets include <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">silane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifa.a281_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ifa.a281-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Supercritical fluids, although not liquids, do share various properties with liquids. Underneath the thick atmospheres of the planets <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, it is expected that these planets are composed of oceans of hot high-density fluid mixtures of water, ammonia and other volatiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosis.05179_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosis.05179-85">[85]</a></sup> The gaseous outer layers of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> transition smoothly into oceans of <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pss.1999_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-pss.1999-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang03_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang03-87">[87]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">atmosphere of Venus</a> is 96.5% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, which is a supercritical fluid at its surface.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline"
